  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_010a7251-2725-49c5-81fe-51903f129557'
      });
    }

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/22225577-rachel-winters"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1373899103i/22225577._UY100_CR25,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/22225577-rachel-winters">Rachel Winters</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Achilles is Hercule's "twin." I just finished rereading this book because I'm a big AC fan, and I realized I had not read it in years. Now, I know why<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Achilles is Hercule's "twin." I just finished rereading this book because I'm a big AC fan, and I realized I had not read it in years. Now, I know why. It's possibly among AC's 10 worst books and by far, in my opinion, the worst Poirot/Hastings book. It is third or fourth at the beginning of her career and I suspect she was trying things out. She had not decided exactly what to do with Hastings so she portrays him as a bumbling idiot. She got the nuance better in later books when she went with the lovable fool persona. I skimmed a lot. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerFooter'>

      $('span_class_gr-hyperlink_sort_order_span_tip_172').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>    </div>

<div id="review_201695600" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201695600" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/184521-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465220302p2/184521.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201695600?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/184521-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201695600" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer858930923165116941">This is a weird one. Apparently (I looked this up, because the book was so weird) Christie wrote a series of short stories about Poirot for some magazine and these stories were mashed together into this book. You can sort of tell that something like this is up, because the mashing didn't work all that well and things still seem a little disjointed. <br/>But really, the main problem here is that Poirot is fighting a team of supervillains, and he uses disguises and smoke bombs and has to find their sec</span>
  <span id="freeText858930923165116941" style="display:none">This is a weird one. Apparently (I looked this up, because the book was so weird) Christie wrote a series of short stories about Poirot for some magazine and these stories were mashed together into this book. You can sort of tell that something like this is up, because the mashing didn't work all that well and things still seem a little disjointed. <br />But really, the main problem here is that Poirot is fighting a team of supervillains, and he uses disguises and smoke bombs and has to find their secret underground lair and the whole thing is just...odd. It comes off like Poirot fan fiction. I mean, he's the same character and all that, but the things he is doing are just not what I want Poirot to do. And the supervillains make no sense. They are a Chinese mastermind, a French woman scientist, an American billionaire, and an English master of disguise. How did these people get together? Why are they a team? Do they have any motive to speak of? They just want to take over the world and cause anarchy, for some reason. Sometimes Christie has characters talk of this "big four" wanting to cause world revolution "like in Russia", but that fails to clear things up. Are they communists? Why would the billionaire be a communist? <br />I don't want Poirot to fight supervillains. I want him in little English towns, ferreting out murderers who have motives. I like it when priggish British ladies say things like "What on earth are you on about, you batty little man?! Are you accusing me of murder?" to which Poirot says things like "You have, as they say, caught the bird in the bush, Madame. This is exactly what I accuse you of." And then he and Hastings drink sherry or something. </span>
  <a data-text-id="858930923165116941" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2936513222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9562973984659464964">Written in 1927, Hercule Poirot and Mr. Hastings pursue the existence of “The Big Four” – a Chinese man, an American man, a Frenchwoman, and an Englishman known as “the Destroyer” – in a quest to sideline their leadership in a conspiracy of global control.<br/><br/>Papers are discovered that refer to <i>
  <b>”. . . some powerful wireless installation – a concentration of wireless energy far beyond anything so far attempted, and capable of focusing a beam of great intensity upon some given spot.”</b>
</i> There is also</span>
  <span id="freeText9562973984659464964" style="display:none">Written in 1927, Hercule Poirot and Mr. Hastings pursue the existence of “The Big Four” – a Chinese man, an American man, a Frenchwoman, and an Englishman known as “the Destroyer” – in a quest to sideline their leadership in a conspiracy of global control.<br /><br />Papers are discovered that refer to <i>
  <b>”. . . some powerful wireless installation – a concentration of wireless energy far beyond anything so far attempted, and capable of focusing a beam of great intensity upon some given spot.”</b>
</i> There is also a reference to harnessing atomic energy.<br /><br />Where in the world, in the year 1927, would Agatha Christie have come across such a possibility? It boggles my mind.<br /><br />Hercule Poirot and Mr. Hastings have several confrontations with members of the Big Four during the course of this novel, as they realize that Poirot must be eliminated if they are to succeed in their mission to control the world and all the entities that make the world turn. Along the way, Poirot and Hastings also need to deal with the “collateral damage” that the Big Four leave in their wake.<br /><br />Agatha Christie continues to surprise me with the range of her mysteries and their complexity. Add in the quirks of some of her characters and a light touch of humour among heart-racing tensions, and this novel comes out a winner.</span>

<div id="review_446134406" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/446134406" />
    <a title="Evgeny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny"><img alt="Evgeny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544797852p2/9340136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/446134406?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=hercule-poirot">hercule-poirot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer446134406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7314576111202951153"><b>Review updated on May 1, 2019.</b><br/><br/>Before the review I just want to ask a couple of questions. What was The Queen of Mystery smoking? It this stuff still available in 21st century?<br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556752481i/27442244._SX540_.jpg' alt='Cheech and Chong' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>On to the review: the only reason I can justify the existence of this abomination is that it is a parody (I hope it is) of Sherlock Holmes vs. Prof. Moriarty duel of wits. Basically, there is an international crime cartel led by 4 people the last of whom is a ruthless killer whose identity is unknown. It is up to Poirot an</span>
  <span id="freeText7314576111202951153" style="display:none"><b>Review updated on May 1, 2019.</b><br><br>Before the review I just want to ask a couple of questions. What was The Queen of Mystery smoking? It this stuff still available in 21st century?<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556752481i/27442244._SX540_.jpg" alt="Cheech and Chong" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>On to the review: the only reason I can justify the existence of this abomination is that it is a parody (I hope it is) of Sherlock Holmes vs. Prof. Moriarty duel of wits. Basically, there is an international crime cartel led by 4 people the last of whom is a ruthless killer whose identity is unknown. It is up to Poirot and Hastings to track him down. It is a collection of seemingly unrelated cases with guy #4 always being the culprit. I also need to mention that the most part of the last case is taken almost word-by-word from first half of Sherlock Holmes' The Final Problem with substitution by names. While the original is a very tragic story, this one reads as a buffoonery. <br><br>Sherlock Holmes as an action hero works. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556752853i/27442261._SX540_.jpg" alt="Action hero" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Hercule Poirot does not. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556752853i/27442262._SX540_.jpg" alt="Wimpy" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>To add insult to the injury when Poirot became an unbeatable guy (physically) he experienced a drop in IQ - something to be expected I guess. A couple of times he overlooked very obvious things. At times the stories themselves become stupid. <br><br>The book is a disgrace to Hercule Poirot. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556753440i/27442276.jpg" alt="Disgrace" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I am a big fan of him, and the next worst Poirot story gets solid 4 stars from me, but try as I might I cannot bring myself to give this one more than 1 star. Before reading this book I thought Agatha Christie can do not wrong. The Big Four quickly cured me of this silly idea. At least this is not the worst from one of the world's best mystery writers: this dubious distinction goes to Passenger to Frankfurt which is an incomprehensible mess. <br><br>Try to avoid The Big Four if you have not read it before, even if you are a die-hard fan.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7314576111202951153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16220560571598473278">Once upon a time, Agatha Christie stayed up way too late, drank her way through three bottles of Chardonnay and watched a Sean Connery James Bond film. As she was going to bed, some time about 3am, her eyes rested for a moment on her typewriter. I would not be in the least surprised if that was all she remembered about this book. For shame, Agatha. This is exactly the same reason I'm not allowed whisky and Twitter on the same evening any more.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007234516" href="/book/show/923228.The_Big_Four">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=det-agatha-christie">det-agatha-christie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=detectives-challenge-hp-2018-20">detectives-challenge-hp-2018-20</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=zz-owned-books">zz-owned-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2276911719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10722975381810361357">Now apparently (allegedly ?) this book was a "mash-up" of short stories that ended up as a novel, and some people have criticised the book for that very fact. Personally if I hadn't been told this "fact" I wouldn't have guessed, although, it is set out as a series of little mysteries. I quite liked this format as I lived with the progression of the overall storyline, as Hastings and Poirot battled the Big Four through a series of adventures.<br/><br/>Strangely I had never read this book before, I don't kn</span>
  <span id="freeText10722975381810361357" style="display:none">Now apparently (allegedly ?) this book was a "mash-up" of short stories that ended up as a novel, and some people have criticised the book for that very fact. Personally if I hadn't been told this "fact" I wouldn't have guessed, although, it is set out as a series of little mysteries. I quite liked this format as I lived with the progression of the overall storyline, as Hastings and Poirot battled the Big Four through a series of adventures.<br /><br />Strangely I had never read this book before, I don't know how I missed it, but I really enjoyed it. And it brings me up to date with "Jessica's" challenge. <br /><br />I think I've written this review back to front as I'm now talking about the book. I am surprised that this is only Hercule Poirot Number 5, it just seemed that Poirot was old (ish) and Hastings was still living in Argentina, which I thought happened late on in their relationship. But that was all just my impression. <br />All said and done it was a very enjoyable read (and yes I still "see" David Suchet and Hugh Fraser, and always will)</span>
  <a data-text-id="10722975381810361357" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_24699885" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24699885" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/644865-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1219075423p2/644865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24699885?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/644865-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer24699885&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24699885" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12275021481818306175">This is possibly the silliest book Christie ever wrote. It's full of Trilateral Commission conspiracy theories, evil scientists developing powerful beams that can wreak untold damage, and a hidden fortress inside a mountain. Yes, really. The only thing it lacked was sharks with fricking lasers attached to their heads.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2892066688" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2892066688" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2892066688?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-c-d">read-authors-c-d</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2892066688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15067038534751050255">About half way through this novel, I began to wonder just why there were so many characters. Agatha Christie often tried to bemuse her readers, by having a largish cast of characters to provide plenty of red herrings, but in this one, she really seemed to have taken this to the limit. It seemed as if in each chapter, we dispensed with most of those in the earlier ones, and were introduced to others. The only linking feature seemed to be that of <b>The Big Four</b>. It was more like reading a series of </span>
  <span id="freeText15067038534751050255" style="display:none">About half way through this novel, I began to wonder just why there were so many characters. Agatha Christie often tried to bemuse her readers, by having a largish cast of characters to provide plenty of red herrings, but in this one, she really seemed to have taken this to the limit. It seemed as if in each chapter, we dispensed with most of those in the earlier ones, and were introduced to others. The only linking feature seemed to be that of <b>The Big Four</b>. It was more like reading a series of short stories, than a novel, I thought.<br /><br />On completion of the novel, I googled to see when and where the author was when she had written it. Often this proves interesting, and one can see how dramatic situations, or current events in an author’s personal life, have influenced their writing. So I was surprised, but oddly satisfied, to learn that the novel of <b>The Big Four</b>, reviewed here, had begun as twelve short stories (or eleven in the US), each of which had been separately published. The twelve linked short stories first appeared in <i>“The Sketch”</i> magazine in 1924 under the series sub-heading, <i>“The Man who was No. 4”</i>, before being amalgamated into one narrative. I shall list them here, along with the relevant chapters in the book, for comparison. They were:<br /><br /><i>The Unexpected Guest: (chapters 1 and 2 of the book – The Unexpected Guest / The Man from the Asylum.)<br />The Adventure of the Dartmoor Bungalow: (chapters 3 and 4 of the book – We hear more about Li Chang Yen / The Importance of a Leg of Mutton.)<br />The Lady on the Stairs: (chapters 5 and 6 of the book – Disappearance of a Scientist / The Woman on the Stairs).<br />The Radium Thieves: (chapter 7 of the book)<br />In the House of the Enemy: (chapter 8 of the book)<br />The Yellow Jasmine Mystery: (chapters 9 and 10 of the book – The Yellow Jasmine Mystery / We investigate at Croftlands).<br />The Chess Problem: (chapter 11 of the book – A Chess Problem).<br />The Baited Trap: (chapters 12 and 13 of the book – The Baited Trap / The Mouse walks in).<br />The Adventure of the Peroxide Blonde: (chapter 14 of the book – The Peroxide Blonde).<br />The Terrible Catastrophe: (chapter 15 of the book)<br />The Dying Chinaman: (chapter 16 of the book)<br />The Crag in the Dolomites: (chapters 17 and 18 of the book – Number Four wins the trick / In the Felsenlabyrinth).</i><br /><br />The resulting novel, comprising 18 chapters, was published in 1927, and is number five in the Poirot oeuvre. The beginning is exciting and intriguing. Captain Arthur Hastings has returned to England after 18 months, leaving his wife behind in Argentina. He pays a surprise visit to his old friend Hercule Poirot, but is astonished to find Poirot ironically on the verge of leaving for South America! Poirot tells him that he has been summoned to Rio de Janeiro by his newest client, the millionaire businessman, Abe Ryland.<br /><br />However, he is delayed slightly when a stranger bursts in through Poirot’s bedroom. The man is emaciated, and covered from head to toe with dust and mud. He is emaciated, as though he has been long imprisoned, with little access to food. Collapsing on the floor, he says Poirot’s name, and writes the number 4.<br /><br />Hastings mentions the <i>“Big Four”</i>, and gaining a little strength, the man begins to talk about about the international crime cartel of that name. He tells Poirot and Hastings that Number 1 is a Chinese political mastermind named Li Chang Yen. This man is the real brains behind the “Big Four”. Number 2 is usually not named but is represented by a dollar sign, or two stripes and a star, so he is probably American and he represents wealth. Number 3 is a Frenchwoman, and Number 4 is referred to as <i>“The Destroyer”</i> and is otherwise unknown.<br /><br />The man is clearly in need of medical help, so Poirot and Hastings summon a doctor who examines the patient and prescribes rest. They leave the man in the care of Poirot’s housekeeper, and set off to catch a train to Southampton, so that Poirot can fulfil his next assignment. However, during the journey, Poirot realises that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ his prospective employer Abe Ryland was probably number 2, and his South American mission was merely an excuse to get him out of the way. The two leap off the train, and return to London, only to find their visitor dead. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Another stranger arrives, who claims to work for a lunatic asylum, and to be in search of an escaped inmate. He identifies the dead man as <i>“Mayerling”</i> the man in question, and leaves. However, Poirot is suspicious. Has the man really died from exhaustion? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ He examines Mayerling and announces that he had been gagged, and then poisoned by inhaling <i>“prussic acid”</i> (hydrogen cyanide). Poirot realises that the murderer was the man from the asylum. He was “The Destroyer”,  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and sure enough the hands of the lounge clock have been turned to 4 o’clock, as a sign.<br /><br />Already we are hooked into the premise of the novel, and this introductory sequence has effectively introduced us to the main villains of the piece. But from then on, the story becomes more disjointed, with a confusing number of characters who star briefly before disappearing again, and it is clear that the reason for this is because the “novel” is ten short stories loosely and clunkily connected.<br /><br /><b>The Big Four</b> is not as popular as many of the other novels which feature our favourite portly Belgian detective and his well-meaning loyal companion, Captain Arthur Hastings. In fact it is unusual in another way too, fitting better into the genre of spy stories than detective stories, as it is a tale of international intrigue and espionage.<br /><br />Personally I felt that Hercule Poirot and Captain Hastings behaved atypically in this novel. It stretched my credulity to the utmost, to imagine the duo jumping from a moving train – accompanied by their luggage – or to have one of the daring duo suspended over an open trapdoor in a cellar in London’s Chinatown, within seconds of being plunged to his death into the icy river Thames.<br /><br />I did attempt to go with the different portrayal of these two as dashing <i>“Boy’s Own”</i> heroes. And when one of them asked for a <i>“last cigarette”</i> before being put to death, I did not baulk too much at the unlikelihood of the cigarette chosen by the villain, being precisely the one containing a poison dart. I went along too with the assurance given by our gallant chap, that this could be directed at will to immediately kill the criminal mastermind. (I quite enjoyed the experience of vividly seeing a comic-book character in my mind’s eye!)<br /><br />No, what really took the biscuit was the long speech explaining all this, made by our hero, <u>whilst keeping the cigarette positioned carefully in his mouth to enable a direct hit</u>! Just try miming this for yourself, and the absurdity soon hits you. It reminded me of grand opera, or a Shakespeare soliloquy, rather than a spy novel. And it reads like a penny dreadful.<br /><br />Apart from the “Boys Own” feel, which jars with the characters of Poirot and Hastings, the resulting jaunty account of daring escapades is just far too complicated. Take the number of locations. Plenty of novels have a switch between locations once, or maybe twice. Or perhaps different characters move in between different locations. This we can follow.<br /><br />But in <b>The Big Four</b>, we start off in Hercule Poirot’s apartment in London. Then we have an abortive railway trip to Southampton and a return to London. Then we visit a village in Devon, somewhere near Dartmoor. Then we seem to suddenly find ourselves miles away, in Chobham, Surrey.<br /><br />Not content with this, we speed across to France, to the Passy area of Paris – and then rush back to England to Hatton Chase, (wherever that is – I suspect this is fictional) apparently the seat of the Duke of Loamshire, and then rush off to Market Hanford, in Worcestershire.<br /><br />Phew! Have we finished? Not at all. We then return to where we started – to London – and through mysterious annals to London’s Chinatown. We must not forget an essential visit to a restaurant in Soho, and another trip abroad to Belgium: there are two trips on ocean-going ships.<br /><br />Surely this is enough! But no. There is a final railway trip from London to Paris, and from there to the South Tyrol in Italy, bordering Austria.<br /><br />Why? Who in their right mind would think this to be a good structure for a shortish novel? But once you know the provenance of the novel, the answer is obvious. It has only come about like this, because twelve stories have been cobbled together.<br /><br /><b>The Big Four</b> was published a year after the inspired classic murder mystery <i>“The Murder of Roger Ackroyd”</i> and remains rightly overshadowed by its predecessor. Agatha Christie was already deeply affected by the death of her mother earlier in 1926, and the breakdown of her marriage to Archibald Christie. It was her brother-in-law, Campbell Christie, who suggested collating her short stories from <i>“The Sketch”</i> magazine into a novel, rather than undergo the strain of composing a completely new novel. He actually helped Agatha Christie to adapt and edit them into one long sequence. In fact, all he really did was to slightly alter the beginning and end of each story, to join them up. The result is as you would expect.<br /><br />It is on record that around this time, a novel was offered for publication to The Bodley Head and was rejected. It is quite likely to have been <b>The Big Four</b>. Agatha Christie also changed her publisher to William Collins, Sons., at this time, which seems to fit. <b>The Big Four</b> was published just a few weeks after Agatha Christie had famously disappeared without trace for ten days in December . This true life mystery ensured that whatever she wrote would inevitably have huge sales.<br /><br />Many years later, in 1942, Christie wrote to her agent:<br /><br /><i>“I have been, once, in a position where I wanted to write just for the sake of money coming in and when I felt I couldn’t – it is a nerve wracking feeling … That was the time I had to produce that rotten book “The Big Four” and had to force myself [again] in “The Mystery of the Blue Train””</i>: the following one in her canon, and which she had found an ordeal to write. Of that one, in Agatha Christie’s autobiography, she stated that she <i>“always hated it”</i>.<br /><br />I do find myself wondering, with both these novels, whether Agatha Christie found that she could not dissociate her devastating experiences of life at that time, from her two novels, and this is why she continued to detest them so vehemently. I also feel that, because of this, if she had written them at a different time, they would have been much better books!<br /><br />The BBC have adapted every single story about Hercule Poirot for television, over many years. David Suchet plays the lead character, and for many people he simply “is” Hercule Poirot. However, it is interesting that <b>The Big Four</b>, although an early novel, was one of the last to be adapted. Also noticeable is that the resulting episode is only very loosely based on the novel.<br /><br />The much respected and talented writer, actor and director Mark Gatiss, who reworked the novel into an adaptation, considered it to be <i>“an almost unadaptable mess</i>”. He found it necessary to remove a great deal of the novel, including the death of <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Mayerling in Poirot’s flat, the radium exploit involving Madame Olivier, Captain Hastings’s time as Abe Ryland’s secretary, and Poirot’s pretence at a twin, Achille, as a diversionary subterfuge. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Only three of the dozen stories are actually included: the leg of mutton case, the chess murder, and the yellow jasmine (or in the TV adaptation, gelsemine) mystery. Such a drastic removal of three quarters of the plot, means that the cast of characters is pared down to a manageable level, and I personally feel that he included three of the best stories. Even so though, Mark Gatiss found it necessary to miss out one major character, Countess Vera Rossakoff, whom Hercule Poirot had encountered before, and who comprised an essential part of the novel.<br /><br />Perhaps though, the most significant changes were to the four villains themselves. In the novel, all four members of the Big Four: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Li Chang Yen, Madame Olivier, Abe Ryland and Albert Whalley, alias Claud Darrell <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> are revealed by Poirot to be guilty of their crimes, and aiming at world domination. But in the TV adaptation <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Li Chang Yen, Madame Olivier and Abe Ryland are all innocent, and were deliberately framed by the sole villain, “The Destroyer”, known as Claud Darrell. This is one of several disguises of Albert Whalley, who is a brilliant but insane character actor who had orchestrated the entire Big Four setup.<br /><br />His motive in doing all of this had been to attract the admiration of Flossie Monro, who had told him in the past that he wasn’t good enough for her. He was attempting to make himself into an international celebrity. Since he had ordered Flossie’s murder in the novel,  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> this is a completely different motive, and perhaps not as convincing – although who can say with such a stereotypical “madman”.<br /><br />The ending of the novel and the adaptation are completely different, although Mark Gatiss has clearly tried to keep to the feel of the original stories, by including similar aspects such as <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Poirot staging his own death  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, but writing a completely different scenario for it.<br /><br />In the novel, the dramatic ending takes place in <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the elaborate underground headquarters of the Big Four – The Felsenlabyrinth, within a mountain in the South Tyrol. Poirot engineers a deadly explosion, from which he, Hastings, and the Countess escape. She has switched sides, because Poirot tirelessly searched for her child whom she believed dead. Poirot proved to the Countess that her child was still alive, by showing her a photograph of him. <br /><br />In the adaptation, Claud Darrell tricked Poirot into going to an old repertory theatre, where he and Flossie had acted as young adults. Claud Darrell attempts to set off some dynamite, but Poirot persuades him to dismantle the explosive, using not killing Flossie as a reason. So Mark Gatiss has used the same sort of idea, but substituted one of the other original characters for that of the Countess Vera Rossakoff, whom he had cut out of the dramatisation. On trying to shoot Poirot, a journalist called Lawrence Boswell Tysoe, drops a safety curtain on Claud Darrell. The other three escape. (Both this journalist, and Miss Lemon, Poirot’s secretary are original characters for the adaptation. They are not in the novel.)  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />To be honest, I enjoyed the adaptation of <b>The Big Four</b> far more than the original novel. Sine Mark Gatiss made an exciting drama from a quarter of the short stories, it seems a shame that the other nine stories were not also adapted into further episodes. There is enough material here for four exciting episodes about the Big Four, and their attempts at world domination, at a time of uncertainty in history, between the two World Wars. But such was not to be.<br /><br />I do not recommend reading this novel, but think the material does merit two stars for ingenuity, and there are some very amusing exchanges between Hercule Poirot and Captain Hastings. However this duo do not really conform to the active heroes needed for the novel, and since the novel ends with Poirot retiring to grow vegetable marrows (as indeed, we see him doing at the beginning of the previous novel, <i>“The Murder of Roger Ackroyd”</i>) the ages are disparate too. <br /><br />But by far the biggest drawback is that forming the stories into such a hotch-potch of a novel, means that it does not even achieve the Goodreads three star rating of “I liked it”, for me. The individual stories themselves are still available, and it would probably be a far better reading experience to read them separately, instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15067038534751050255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2411058416" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2411058416" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5837456-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372225128p2/5837456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2411058416?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/5837456-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/35420008-the-big-four">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2411058416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11121032112245812252">This novel was published in 1927. It follows, “The Murder of Roger Ackroyd,” and it also followed Christie’s notorious eleven day disappearance; an event which haunted her all her life. Devastated by the death of her mother, and the breakdown of her marriage, her brother in law suggested that she re-work some short stories, previously published in “The Sketch,” an American magazine. Due to the publicity surrounding her disappearance, “The Big Four,” was a huge success – much more so than, “The M</span>
  <span id="freeText11121032112245812252" style="display:none">This novel was published in 1927. It follows, “The Murder of Roger Ackroyd,” and it also followed Christie’s notorious eleven day disappearance; an event which haunted her all her life. Devastated by the death of her mother, and the breakdown of her marriage, her brother in law suggested that she re-work some short stories, previously published in “The Sketch,” an American magazine. Due to the publicity surrounding her disappearance, “The Big Four,” was a huge success – much more so than, “The Murder of Roger Ackroyd.” However, despite this, the critical acclaim for Roger Ackroyd has grown (quite rightly), while, “The Big Four,” has been criticised as being disjointed. Robert Barnard, for example, stated that it was, “cobbled together,” “demeaning to Poirot,” and “pretty dreadful.”<br /><br />Despite these criticisms, though, “The Big Four,” is, to my mind, a lot of fun and showed just how good Christie was under pressure. This is more thriller than mystery and it may have been, “cobbled together,” from different short stories, but it has a lot to delight fans. It begins with Hastings returning from Argentina, on a surprise visit; only to discover that Poirot is about to leave the country. However, Poirot’s journey is delayed by a mysterious stranger and, before long, Poirot and Hastings are embroiled in trying to uncover the Big Four – a criminal gang responsible for illegal activities worldwide. <br /><br />What follows is non-stop action, as Hastings and Poirot follow leads, investigate murders, find themselves in danger and do what they can to discover the identity of these criminal masterminds. The relationship between Poirot and Hastings is both warm and humorous. I listened to the Audible version of this, read by Hugh Fraser, who played Hastings in the television series and I enjoyed his narration. Yes, sometimes the plot gets a bit silly, but I loved the real sense of affection that Poirot displays for his friend, Hastings loyalty and the brief appearances of Japp. Although it isn’t Christie’s best, it is better than most author’s could have come up with. Sadly, it is one of only two Poirot novels which has no dedication, possibly showing how alone, or dispirited, she felt at that time. Even at such a low point, she created a fun, fast-moving novel, with a lot of recommend it to Poirot fans and, as such, it is certainly worth reading. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11121032112245812252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7543693609143054016" style="display:none">The big four (Hercule Poirot #5), 1927, Agatha Christie<br />The Big Four is a work of detective fiction by Agatha Christie, first published in the UK by William Collins &amp; Sons on 27 January 1927 and in the US by Dodd, Mead and Company later in the same year. It features Hercule Poirot, Arthur Hastings, and Inspector (later, Chief Inspector) Japp. The UK edition retailed at seven shillings and six pence and the US edition at $2.00.<br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: هجدهم سپتامبر سال 2011 میلادی<br />عنوان: چهار غول بزرگ؛ آگاتا کریستی؛ مترجم: مجتبی عبدالله نژاد؛ تهران، هرمس، کتابهای کارآگاه، 1390؛ در 220 ص، شابک: 9789643637699؛ موضوع: داستانهای کارآگاهی و پلیسی از نویسندگان انگلیسی - قرن 20 م<br />اگر کتاب را خوانده اید و میخواهید بخوانید، ادامه ی این نگاشته را نخوانید. عنوان: چهار قدرت بزرگ؛ را در جاهای دیگر به جای چهار غول بزرگ برگزیده اند؛ یک رمان جنایی اثر بانو آگاتا کریستی ست که در آن هرکول پوآرو و آرتور هستینگز نقش دارند. این کتاب نخستین بار در انگلستان توسط انتشارات ویلیام کولینز و در بیست و هفتم ژآنویه سال 1827 میلادیمنتشر شد و در همان سال در آمریکا. قیمت نسخه انگلیسی آن هفت شلینگ و شش پنی بود. و در آمریکا $2.00. سبک این رمان با دیگر کارهای آگاتا کریتسی متفاوت بوده و چند جنایت که توسط یک سازمان بزرگ روی می‌دهد را روایت می‌کند. داستان از چند قتل تشکیل می‌شود که همگی کار یک سازمان مخوف به نام چهار قدرت بزرگ هستند؛ که چهار شخصیت اصلی دارند: یک چینی، یک زن دانشمند فرانسوی، یک ثروتمند آمریکایی، و یک بازیگر تئاتر؛ که قتل‌ها به عهده اوست. انگار داستان را لو دادم. ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13858160520347586348">This feels like Agatha Christie's ode to Sherlock Holmes. The story is told through the 'Watson' character, a friend of Poirot and we see how amazed and blundering he is compared to Poirot. The villain in this story is a crime syndicate called the Big 4 and they are supposed to be super villains. Each of the 4 come from different countries.<br/><br/>Poriot must bring down this hidden cabal that governments don't believe exists. The story is solid, it simply doesn't feel original. I do enjoy reading about </span>
  <span id="freeText13858160520347586348" style="display:none">This feels like Agatha Christie's ode to Sherlock Holmes. The story is told through the 'Watson' character, a friend of Poirot and we see how amazed and blundering he is compared to Poirot. The villain in this story is a crime syndicate called the Big 4 and they are supposed to be super villains. Each of the 4 come from different countries.<br /><br />Poriot must bring down this hidden cabal that governments don't believe exists. The story is solid, it simply doesn't feel original. I do enjoy reading about Poirot and I want to read more of her books about this detective. He certainly thinks highly of himself.<br /><br />This is one of Agatha's earlier works and I think she grows as an author. It's a good story. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2046363289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10179336425610122101"><i>‘But Poirot— why?’</i><br/><br/>This is possibly the most appropriate quotation I can choose to describe the book. <br/> <br/>The Big Four has one of the silliest plots of Christie's books which is based on Poirot and Hastings engaging in international crime and espionage - much in the same way that Holmes and Watson do,just in a more believable way. Where Holmes is reserved enough to carry off disguises and under-cover work, Poirot's flamboyance has him stick out like a sore thumb all over the place, and Christie's at</span>
  <span id="freeText10179336425610122101" style="display:none"><i>‘But Poirot— why?’</i><br /><br />This is possibly the most appropriate quotation I can choose to describe the book. <br /> <br />The Big Four has one of the silliest plots of Christie's books which is based on Poirot and Hastings engaging in international crime and espionage - much in the same way that Holmes and Watson do,just in a more believable way. Where Holmes is reserved enough to carry off disguises and under-cover work, Poirot's flamboyance has him stick out like a sore thumb all over the place, and Christie's attempts at making us believe that Hastings, or indeed anyone else, does not recognise him are just ludicrous.<br /> <br />The book was written in 1927 and is the first of Christie's novels that show her making an attempt at writing an espionage / crime thriller with an international setting. As ludicrous as this book is, it is still better than some of the other attempts that were to follow (I'm looking at you Passenger to Frankfurt!), but it is surprising that the book did not turn readers off Christie altogether. <br /> <br />Not only does the plot not hold any water, the writing is just unbelievably bad - culminating in an ending that is just ... wow, so ridiculous. Apparently, Dame Agatha got stuck and basically just hit the big red button. That is all I'm going to say about that (just in case someone really wants to read the book).<br /> <br />For the rest, ... Oh, boy. How many times can Hasting's get knocked out? Or knocked around by Poirot?<br /> <br />Seriously, for many parts of the book, I rooted for Hasting's shoving Poirot off a cliff.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10179336425610122101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4839159591228097510">This book is a weird Hercule Poirot story. Not the usual mystery murder of a person from the upper class members of society. More like a thriller or a quicker pace mystery of Sherlock Holmes. What do you think about a story where Hercule Poirot against a crime organisation that aim for worldly power?   <span id="freeText4839159591228097510" style="display:none">This book is a weird Hercule Poirot story. Not the usual mystery murder of a person from the upper class members of society. More like a thriller or a quicker pace mystery of Sherlock Holmes. What do you think about a story where Hercule Poirot against a crime organisation that aim for worldly power? If that premise is not bothering you, please proceed.<br /><br />I could still give it a three star until the middle part. Alas, the ending is not so satisfying. Without spoiler, that's all I can say for now.<br /><br />Not exactly don't like this novel, but it was pretty close to 1 star. If I am a devout for <a href="http://www.thrillingdetective.com/trivia/triv288.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Van Dine rules</a>, I would give this book 1 star. This novel definitely broke rule number 13.<br /><br />(Yes yes, I know the author broke these rules several times. But usually the author broke the rule beautifully. If you know another novel that broke the rules worse than this novel, please inform me) </span>

    <a title="Poonam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17656456-poonam"><img alt="Poonam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520691179p2/17656456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1776166390?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Poonam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Poonam" href="/user/show/17656456-poonam">Poonam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17656456-poonam?shelf=mystery-thriller-suspense">mystery-thriller-suspense</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1776166390" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4467711707975941997"><b>2.5 stars</b><br/>This being the 5th book in the Poirot series is very different from any other Poirot book or in fact different to any other Christie book that I have read till date.<br/><br/>Instead of a cozy setting of a house, village or hand few of suspicious people with multiple red herrings, this is about an <b>
  <i>International Gang - Big Four</i>
</b>. This famous gang comprises of 4 people and we know the identity of one of the members right away.<br/><br/>We come to know the identity of the remaining members as and when the </span>
  <span id="freeText4467711707975941997" style="display:none"><b>2.5 stars</b><br />This being the 5th book in the Poirot series is very different from any other Poirot book or in fact different to any other Christie book that I have read till date.<br /><br />Instead of a cozy setting of a house, village or hand few of suspicious people with multiple red herrings, this is about an <b>
  <i>International Gang - Big Four</i>
</b>. This famous gang comprises of 4 people and we know the identity of one of the members right away.<br /><br />We come to know the identity of the remaining members as and when the story progresses. There are multiple short stories in this book where Hercule Poirot faces the Big Four in different mind games.<br /><b>
  <i>"I had no suspicion- at least hardly any suspicion. Yes, but for my quick eyes, the eyes of a cat, Hercule Poirot might now be crushed out of existence- a terrible calamity?"</i>
</b><br /><br />As usual we have Hastings as a loyal but sometimes naive, sometimes bumbling side-kick.<br /><b>
  <i>"How marvelous is my friend Hastings! He knows everything- but everything!"</i>
</b><br /><br />This book at the start had very strong Sherlock Holmes- Dr.Watson vibes which turned into Bond movie vibes by the end.<br /><br />Somehow, I am glad that Christie reverted to her cozy mystery in this series rather than continuing on this action packed international thriller as I definitely enjoyed her other books more than this.<br /><br />But I still enjoyed Poirot and his little grey cells!<br /><b>
  <i>"Poirot has his virtues, but modesty is not one of them."</i>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="4467711707975941997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1671712841" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1671712841" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1671712841?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=mystery-detective-thriller">mystery-detective-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1671712841" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16448437144582518514">If you are looking into reading one Christie/Poirot book to check out what they are like, don't choose this one. This has to be one of Christie's worst efforts, a "departure," though only her fifth Poirot book. Maybe calling it an experiment would work, though that would be generous. I looked into background about the book and I learned that in this year Christie's mother died and she discovered her husband was having an affair, so their marriage was ending. She was as a result strapped for cash</span>
  <span id="freeText16448437144582518514" style="display:none">If you are looking into reading one Christie/Poirot book to check out what they are like, don't choose this one. This has to be one of Christie's worst efforts, a "departure," though only her fifth Poirot book. Maybe calling it an experiment would work, though that would be generous. I looked into background about the book and I learned that in this year Christie's mother died and she discovered her husband was having an affair, so their marriage was ending. She was as a result strapped for cash, and stressed out, unable to get a proper novel together, so her brother suggested she mash-up twelve stories she had written somehow into one novel. This is the result:<br /><br />*Many forgettable characters, too many to keep track of. Bad idea.<br /><br />*Four principal evil villains. Instead of one. And not linked in any way! It's not a series, it's a single novel. Bad idea.<br /><br />*A complicated plot with a twist ending, of course, you expect that, but it's too complicated to care about, confusing, actually, and not that much of a surprise when it all works out.<br /><br />*It's an international thriller, a world stage, versus the small intimate settings Poirot usually works in. Bad idea.<br /><br />*The dreaded Hastings returns, alas,to further muddy the already murky water. Bad idea.<br /><br />*We get to entertain these questions: Is Poirot dead?! Does he have a twin? Bad ideas.<br /><br />I would say this is a pretty bad book, for her or anyone, but I would also say that she is still early in her career, she is trying out different things, she's experimenting, so I give her a break. Many of those things she tries in the book didn't work, but she is still working on developing that Poirot character here, and that is not a complete waste of time to watch, the master developing her craft.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16448437144582518514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1893093801" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1893093801" />
    <a title="Juli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38617426-juli"><img alt="Juli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453642808p2/38617426.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1893093801?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juli" href="/user/show/38617426-juli">Juli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/25003646-the-big-four">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38617426-juli?shelf=read-audiobooks-2020">read-audiobooks-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38617426-juli?shelf=agatha-christie-owned-worn-out-ppbk">agatha-christie-owned-worn-out-ppbk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1893093801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1406603415690948619">For years I wanted to read everything Agatha Christie wrote....every book, every short story and each play. But life is always so busy....and Christie wrote more than 60 detective novels and 14 short story collections. I came to love Christie's books when I was 9 and bought my first Hercule Poirot books at a garage sale. For years, I carried a tattered list of her books I didn't already own in my purse, buying titles to fill in my collection when I could. But I never found time to read most of t</span>
  <span id="freeText1406603415690948619" style="display:none">For years I wanted to read everything Agatha Christie wrote....every book, every short story and each play. But life is always so busy....and Christie wrote more than 60 detective novels and 14 short story collections. I came to love Christie's books when I was 9 and bought my first Hercule Poirot books at a garage sale. For years, I carried a tattered list of her books I didn't already own in my purse, buying titles to fill in my collection when I could. But I never found time to read most of them.....and in the pre-internet age it was difficult to keep track of them all. Some books were published under one title in the UK and another in the US....or some stories weren't available in the US at all. And book listings in the front of paperbacks were not necessarily in order. It just became too Herculean a task .....and life whirled me away in other directions.<br /><br />Until now.<br /><br />Armed with the internet, digital library offerings, and my own book collection, I have finally started my Christie quest. And I'm loving every minute of it!!<br /><br />The Big Four was published in the UK and the US in 1927 and features one of Christie's most popular characters, Hercule Poirot. I was excited to reach this title in my quest because this is my first reading of this Poirot mystery!! I own a tattered paperback from the early 1980s, but never read it. I'm sure I picked up the copy at a thriftshop, garage sale or used book store somewhere and was delighted to cross the title off my Christie shopping list...but then the paperback joined the collection on my shelves and was never enjoyed. I have now happily rectified that situation! I'm really not sure how I missed out on reading this book. It was published just weeks after Christie disappeared for 11 days (December 3-14, 1926). Public interest in her disappearance and eventual discovery at a hotel in Harrogate caused sales of The Big Four to skyrocket. I never knew this interesting fact until now. If I had, I might have read this book long before 2020! As it is, knowing a bit more about the background (the internet can be a wonderful learning tool!) of the story and its timing, made this an even more enjoyable audio book for me. <br /><br />The audio version I listened to is narrated by Hugh Fraser (Harper Audio). Fraser gives a great performance. The unabridged audio is 5 1/2 hours...so it's a relatively easy listen. <br /><br />The Big Four is actually a mashup of several earlier published short stories centering around the diabolical antics of a international group of four criminal masterminds. The stories were first published in the Sketch magazine in the UK from January - March 1924. US publication in Blue Book Magazine followed in March 1927 - January 1928. The 12 short stories are: The Unexpected Guest, The Adventure of the Dartmoor Bungalow, The Lady on the Stairs, The Radium Thieves, In the House of the Enemy, The Yellow Jasmine Mystery, The Chess Problem, The Baited Trap, The Adventure of the Peroxide Blonde, The Terrible Catastrophe, The Dying Chinaman, and the Crag in the Dolomites. Each story was worked into one or two chapters of The Big Four. The stories are all interconnected and assembled as a novel the tales actually flow pretty well. I would never have known the story started out as different short tales had I not learned as much while doing pre-reading research into the book. I can see why Christie went this route.....her personal life at the time was in tatters. Her mother had died, she was deeply depressed, her husband left her for another woman.....who can write a new, glorious murder mystery while perhaps having suicidal, dark, depressed thoughts? She cobbled together prior stories that work perfectly well together....and granted herself some time to work on her personal life. Kudos, Agatha! <br /><br />The book was adapted by the television series Poirot in its final season. <br /><br />The basics: Poirot gets a surprise visit from an old friend, Captain Hastings. But, they don't get to have a relaxing reunion.....the two are pulled into international intrigue as a group of 4 mysterious criminals attempt to further their attempts to gain world dominance. While the concept of taking over the world through nefarious means is now a cheesy plot, back when this book was written the plot wasn't cliche. There were a couple times as I listened to this story that my mind pulled up visions of Dr. Evil, Mojo JoJo and James Bond villains.....but all in all, it is an enjoyable Poirot adventure. A bit dated....but enjoyable. <br /><br />There were spots in this tale that I found a bit ..... racist isn't the term I'm looking for really. Let's say...racially or culturally insensitive. The plot pretty much jumps right into the concept that The Bad Guys must be affiliated with American criminals, Russians or the Chinese. And a few antiquated terms (like coolie) are used. But, the book is nearly 100 years old. Political intrigue tales just don't age very well. The cultural concepts of 1920s England just seem a bit cheesy, cliche and insensitive in 2020. Even so, I still enjoyed Poirot and Hastings sleuthing out the identities of these 4 sneaky, international masterminds. They tried to trick, out maneuver and even kill the master detective. Silly ploy....they were the Diet Coke of Evil....not quite evil enough. ha ha. <br /><br />So glad I finally read this book!! I wish I could read the stories in their original format. I'm going to do some sleuthing of my own and see if I can't ferret out the original tales online just so I can say that I read them. Moving on to the next novel: The Mystery of the Blue Train! (Another Christie novel I haven't read before!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1406603415690948619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2261485353" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2261485353" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7202176-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324164999p2/7202176.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2261485353?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/7202176-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062073877" href="/book/show/9960868-the-big-four">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=1920s">1920s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2261485353" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5023809765815454955">What the <i>hell</i> did I just read?<br/><br/>See that rating? That's a one star rating. Had you asked me a week ago I would have said that Christie did not write one star books. Sure some are of much lesser quality, but one star seems rather much. Had this book been written by some other author with some other character it would have possibly been entertainingly bad enough to receive two stars. Here it is an insult to the characters and a bit of a train wreck.<br/><br/>This is bad... like so bad that if feels like it wa</span>
  <span id="freeText5023809765815454955" style="display:none">What the <i>hell</i> did I just read?<br /><br />See that rating? That's a one star rating. Had you asked me a week ago I would have said that Christie did not write one star books. Sure some are of much lesser quality, but one star seems rather much. Had this book been written by some other author with some other character it would have possibly been entertainingly bad enough to receive two stars. Here it is an insult to the characters and a bit of a train wreck.<br /><br />This is bad... like so bad that if feels like it was written by someone with a vague notion of the Poirot cast of characters, but unfamiliar with what the books were actually like. The plot follows Hercule Poirot as he deals with a group of <i>super-criminals</i> hellbent on <i>world domination</i> and using <i>super-science</i> (not to mention a master of disguise). These super-criminals known as the Big Four are even fronted by a Chinese Mastermind.<br /><br />To say this book is a product of its time is both an understatement and also baffling as it is so utterly bizarre that it doesn't really feel appropriate for any time save for possibly an alternate timeline where Poirot was perhaps a comic book character.<br /><br />Seriously, there is a line in the book, and I am quoting here, that goes: "Yes, you are both in the trap - the trap of the Big Four!" This is not Christie dialogue, this is bad comic book dialogue. <br /><br />You know it's bad when Hastings' makes more sense than any of the other characters with his initial disbelief of the situation. Hell, my usual issues with Hastings were mostly dismissed as not only did he seem completely natural in this bizzaro world we were presented, but he also came off as a borderline voice of reason at times.<br /><br />This is just such a surreal book. It reminded me somewhat of those old RKO serial films like Flash Gordon and Zombies of the Stratosphere (Yes, that last one is real, and featured Leonard Nimoy in an early role) where the characters are always placed in a ridiculous situation that they manage to get out of just in the nick of time, with increasingly silly plots that become tiresome to keep track of from a logical standpoint. This is what Poirot would have been like as adapted in such a format. <br /><br />This is a book with over 30 named characters who come and go in a matter of pages. As for the mysteries (and yes, there are many in here) I kept feeling like I was missing clues as Poirot would make sudden announcements and logic leaps. As I continued reading, I began to suspect that poor plotting was the real culprit. A theory I stand by now that it is all over. <br /><br />This book is so mind-bogglingly bad that I had to do some research, trying to find why it even exists, as in the well ordered world of Hercule Poirot, this book has no place. From what I could gather from a couple of different websites, Christie was in desperate need of money after her divorce. Espionage stories were "in" at the time and the novel was created from a reworked collection of short stories. Christie was apparently of much the same opinion about the book as I was as she is quoted to have referred to it as “that rotten book,” of which I must say she is far too kind. <br /><br />An abysmal 1/5 stars. Not recommended to anyone save those trying to read all of Christie's novels or perhaps those who want to read what would happen if Ed Wood had tried his hand at Poirot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5023809765815454955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3508934153" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3508934153" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3508934153?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=classic-mystery">classic-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3508934153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7809504568574620257">If you want to see Hercule Poirot in the shoes of either Sherlock Holmes or James Bond, <i>The Big Four</i> is one for you. For me, I don't want to see the eccentric little Belgian detective wearing any other's shoes but his own, so you can understand why I'm disappointed. <br/><br/><i>The Big Four</i> is the odd man out in the Poirot series. It is not a murder-mystery story per se, although several murders occur in the continuance of the story. It is not even a mystery I would say, more like a spy story. The great Her</span>
  <span id="freeText7809504568574620257" style="display:none">If you want to see Hercule Poirot in the shoes of either Sherlock Holmes or James Bond, <i>The Big Four</i> is one for you. For me, I don't want to see the eccentric little Belgian detective wearing any other's shoes but his own, so you can understand why I'm disappointed. <br /><br /><i>The Big Four</i> is the odd man out in the Poirot series. It is not a murder-mystery story per se, although several murders occur in the continuance of the story. It is not even a mystery I would say, more like a spy story. The great Hercule Poirot is pitted against the biggest international criminal organization called the "The Big Four" which is creating unrest in the world. Poirot with his faithful friend Captain Hastings and the British and other governments backing him uncover them and leads them to their destruction. But it is not easy as is said. Poirot even had to feign his death to achieve his end. <br /><br />The story is a little too fantastic and Agatha Christie, quite unusual to her character, has filled the story with a pack of absurd and nonsensical events. Yet, I can't say that the story was boring. On the contrary, it was still interesting in its absurdity. My disappointment is that this wasn't a murder-mystery story, the type with which I'm accustomed to connecting Poirot. It is me I know, but I don't want Poirot to be a "Hercules" and save the world, I prefer him as the intelligent detective who solves the baffling murder mysteries. <br /><br />This installment is the 5th in the series and I took it up after reading the 37th because I realized that I've missed reading this and two others along the way. Now I feel, I should have left this alone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7809504568574620257" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">
